st\n}}\n| website             = {{url|http://www.gov.cn/guoqing/2022-10/23/content_5721018.htm|www.gov.cn}} {{in lang|zh}}\n}}\n\n{{Xi Jinping sidebar}}\n\n'''Xi Jinping''' ({{IPAc-en|lang|\u02c8|\u0283|i\u02d0|_|d\u0292|\u026a|n|\u02c8|p|\u026a|\u014b}} {{respell|SHEE|_|jin|PING}}; {{zh|s=\u4e60\u8fd1\u5e73|p=X\u00ed J\u00ecnp\u00edng}}; {{IPAc-cmn|x|i|2|-|j|in|4|-|p|ing|2}}; born 15 June 1953) is a Chinese politician who has served as the [[General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party|general secretary]] of the [[Chinese Communist Party]] (CCP) and [[Chairman of the Central Military Commission (China)|chairman]] of the [[Central Military Commission (China)|Central Military Commission]] (CMC), and thus as the [[paramount leader]] of China, since 2012. Xi has also served as the [[president of the People's Republic of China]] (PRC) since 2013.\n\nThe son of Chinese Communist veteran [[Xi Zhongxun]], Xi was exiled to rural [[Yanchuan County]] as a teenager following his father's purge during the [[Cultural Revolution]]. He lived in a [[yaodong]] in the village of Liangjiahe, [[Shaanxi|Shaanxi province]], where he joined the CCP after several failed attempts and worked as the local [[Chinese Communist Party Committee Secretary|party secretary]]. After studying [[chemical engineering]] at [[Tsinghua University]] as a [[Worker-Peasant-Soldier student|worker-peasant-soldier student]], Xi rose through the ranks politically in China's coastal provinces. Xi was [[governor of Fujian]] from 1999 to 2002, before becoming governor and party secretary of neighboring [[Politics of Zhejiang|Zhejiang]] from 2002 to 2007. Following dismissal of the party secretary of Shanghai, [[Chen Liangyu]], Xi was transferred to replace him for a brief period in 2007. He subsequently joined the [[Politburo Standing Committee of the Chinese Communist Party|Politburo Standing Committee]] (PSC) of the CCP the same year and served as first secretary of the [[Secretariat of the Chinese Communist Party|Central Secretariat]] in October 2007. In 2008, he was designated as [[Hu Jintao]]'s presumed [[Succession of power in the People's Republic of China|successor]] as paramount leader; to that end, Xi was appointed [[Vice President of the People's Republic of China|vice president of the PRC]] and [[Vice Chairman of the Central Military Commission|vice chairman of the CMC]]. He officially received the title of [[leadership core]] from the CCP in 2016.\n\nXi is the first CCP general secretary born after the [[Proclamation of the People's Republic of China|establishment of the PRC]]. Since assuming power, Xi has introduced far-ranging measures to enforce party discipline and to impose internal unity. His [[Anti-corruption campaign under Xi Jinping|anti-corruption campaign]] led to the downfall of prominent incumbent and retired CCP officials, including a [[Zhou Yongkang|former member]] of the PSC. He has also enacted or promoted a more aggressive foreign policy, particularly with regard to [[China\u2013United States relations|China's relations with the U.S]]., the [[nine-dash line]] in the [[South China Sea Islands|South China Sea]], the [[Sino-Indian border dispute]], and the [[political status of Taiwan]]. He has sought to expand China's African and Eurasian influence through the [[Belt and Road Initiative]]. Xi has expanded support for [[state-owned enterprise]]s (SOEs), advanced [[military-civil fusion]], overseen [[Targeted Poverty Alleviation|targeted poverty alleviation]] programs, and has attempted to reform the property sector. He has also promoted \"[[common prosperity]]\", a series of policies designed with stated goal to increase equality, and used the term to justify a broad crackdown and major slew of regulations against the tech and tutoring sectors in 2021. \n\nXi [[Ma\u2013Xi meeting|met]] with Taiwanese president [[Ma Ying-jeou]] in 2015, the first time PRC and Republic of China leaders met, though relations deteriorated after [[Tsai Ing-wen]] of the [[Democratic Progressive Party]] (DPP) won the presidential elections in 2016. He responded to the [[COVID-19 pandemic in mainland China]] with a [[zero-COVID]] approach, continuing it (as of December 2022) even after most other countries had shifted to a [[mitigation strategy]]. Xi also oversaw the passage of a [[Hong Kong national security law|national security law in Hong Kong]], clamping down on political opposition in the city, especially [[Pro-democracy camp (Hong Kong)|pro-democracy activists]].\n\nOften described as an authoritarian leader by political and academic observers, Xi's tenure has included an increase of [[Censorship in China|censorship]] and [[Mass surveillance in China|mass surveillance]], deterioration in [[Human rights in China|human rights]], including the [[Xinjiang internment camps|internment of one million Uyghurs in Xinjiang]] (which some observers have described as part of [[Uyghur genocide|a genocide]]), a [[Xi Jinping's cult of personality|cult of personality]] developing around Xi, and the removal of term limits for the presidency in 2018. Xi's political ideas and principles, known as [[Xi Jinping Thought]], have been incorporated into the [[Constitution of the Chinese Communist Party|party]] and [[Constitution of the People's Republic of China|national constitutions]], and he has emphasized the importance of national security and the need for CCP leadership over the country. As the central figure of the [[Generations of Chinese leadership|fifth generation of leadership]] of the PRC, Xi has centralized institutional power by taking on multiple positions, including chairing the [[National Security Commission of the Chinese Communist Party|National Security Commission]] and new steering committees on economic and social reforms, military restructuring and modernization, and the Internet. He and the CCP [[Central Committee of the Chinese Communist Party|Central Committee]] passed a \"[[Third Resolution of the CPC Central Committee on the Major Achievements and Historical Experience of the Party over the Past Century|historical resolution]]\" in November 2021, the third such resolution after [[Mao Zedong]] and [[Deng Xiaoping]]. In October 2022, Xi secured a third term as CCP General Secretary, the second [[Leader of the Chinese Communist Party|leader of the CCP]] to do so (the other being Mao).\n\n{{TOC limit|3}}\n\n==Early life and education==\nXi Jinping was born in Beijing on 15 June 1953, the second son of [[Xi Zhongxun]] and his wife [[Qi Xin]]. After the [[Proclamation of the People's Republic of China|founding of the PRC]] in 1949, Xi's father held a series of posts, including [[Propaganda Department of the Chinese Communist Party|Party propaganda]] chief, [[Vice Premier of the People's Republic of China|vice-premier]], and [[Vice Chairperson of the Standing Committee of the National People's Congress|vice chairperson of the National People's Congress]].<ref name=\":16\">{{Cite news|url=https://www.rferl.org/a/china-profile-xi-jinping/24764283.html|title=Profile: Chinese Vice President Xi Jinping|date=7 November 2012|publisher=[[Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty]]|access-date=26 August 2013|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131019083336/http://www.rferl.org/content/china-profile-xi-jinping/24764283.html|archive-date=19 October 2013}}</ref> Xi had two older sisters, [[Qi Qiaoqiao|Qiaoqiao]], born in 1949 and An'an ({{zh|c=\u5b89\u5b89|p=\u0100n'\u0101n|labels=no}}), born in 1952.<ref name=\":25\">{{cite news |url=http://news.xinhuanet.com/book/2009-04/28/content_11270872.htm |script-title=zh:\u8207\u4e08\u592b\u7fd2\u4ef2\u52db\u76f8\u4f3458\u5e74 Henan|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130123040758/http://hn.wenweipo.com/news/zhuanti/2012-11-16/23713.html|archive-date=23 January 2013}}</ref>\n\nXi went to the Beijing No. 25 School,<ref>{{cite web |last1=Li |first1=Cheng |title=Xi Jinping's Inner Circle (Part 2: Friends from Xi's Formative Years) |url=https://www.hoover.org/sites/default/files/research/docs/clm44cl.pdf |access-date=15 July 2020 |website=[[Hoover Institution]] |publisher= |pages=6\u201322}}</ref> and then [[Beijing Bayi School]],<ref name=Takahashi>{{cite web|last=Takahashi|first=Tetsushi|url=https://asia.nikkei.com/Spotlight/Beijing-Diary/Connecting-the-dots-of-the-Hong-Kong-law-and-veneration-of-Xi|title=Connecting the dots of the Hong Kong law and veneration of Xi |newspaper=[[Nikkei Shimbun]]|date=1 June 2002|access-date=23 October 2020}}</ref><ref name=\"newyorker2\" /> in the 1960s. He became friends with [[Liu He (politician)|Liu He]], who attended [[Beijing 101 Middle School|Beijing No. 101 School]] in the same district, who later became China's vice-premier and a close advisor to Xi after he became China's paramount leader.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/dec/29/power-behind-the-thrones-10-movers-and-shakers-for-2020|title=The power behind the thrones: 10 political movers and shakers who will shape 2020|last=Tisdall|first=Simon|date=29 December 2019|work=[[The Guardian]]|access-date=16 January 2020|language=en-GB|issn=0029-7712|author-link=Simon Tisdall}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/blessed-by-xi-jinping-the-new-captain-of-chinas-economy-1519688592|title=Who Is 'Uncle He?' The Man in Charge of China's Economy|last=Wei|first=Lingling|date=27 February 2018|work=[[The Wall Street Journal]]|access-date=16 January 2020}}</ref> In 1963, when he was aged 10, his father was purged from the CCP and sent to work in a factory in [[Luoyang]], Henan.{{sfn|Bou\u00e9e|2010|p=93}} In May 1966, the [[Cultural Revolution]] cut short Xi's secondary education when all secondary classes were halted for students to criticise and fight their teachers. Student militants ransacked the Xi family home and one of Xi's sisters, Xi Heping, committed suicide from the pressure.<ref name=\":10\">{{cite news |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2015/09/25/world/asia/xi-jinping-china-cultural-revolution.html |title=Cultural Revolution Shaped Xi Jinping, From Schoolboy to Survivor |last1=Buckley|first1=Chris |last2=Tatlow|first2=Didi Kirsten|date=24 September 2015 |newspaper=[[The New York Times]]|access-date=6 March 2018|url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180305150638/https://www.nytimes.com/2015/09/25/world/asia/xi-jinping-china-cultural-revolution.html |archive-date=5 March 2018 |url-access=limited }}</ref>\n\nLater, his mother was forced to publicly denounce his father, as he was paraded before a crowd as an enemy of the revolution. His father was later imprisoned in 1968 when Xi was aged 15. Without the protection of his father, Xi was sent to work in Liangjiahe Village, [[Wen'anyi]], [[Yanchuan County]], Yan'an, Shaanxi, in 1969 in Mao Zedong's [[Down to the Countryside Movement]].<ref>{{cite web |url=http://v.youku.com/v_show/id_XMzM2NTc1MjAwOA==.html |script-title=zh:\u4e0d\u5fd8\u521d\u5fc3\uff1a\u662f\u4ec0\u4e48\u9020\u5c31\u4e86\u4eca\u5929\u7684\u4e60\u4e3b\u5e2d\uff1f|date=30 January 2018|website=[[Youku]]|language=zh-Hans |trans-title=What Were His Original Intentions? The President Xi of Today |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180130204306/http://v.youku.com/v_show/id_XMzM2NTc1MjAwOA==.html |archive-date=30 January 2018 |access-date=30 January 2018}}</ref> He worked as the [[Chinese Communist Party Committee Secretary|party secretary]] of Liangjiahe, where he lived in a [[yaodong|cave house]].<ref>{{Cite news |last=Lim |first=Louisa |date=9 November 2012 |title=For China's Rising Leader, A Cave Was Once Home |work=[[NPR]] |publisher= |url=https://www.npr.org/2012/11/09/164684418/for-chinas-rising-leader-a-cave-was-once-home?t=1578589261050 |url-status=live |access-date=27 October 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180616053635/https://www.npr.org/2012/11/09/164684418/for-chinas-rising-leader-a-cave-was-once-home |archive-date=16 June 2018}}</ref> According to people who knew him, this experience led him to feel affinity with the rural poor.<ref name=\":29\" /> After a few months, unable to stand rural life, he ran away to Beijing. He was arrested during a crackdown on deserters from the countryside and sent to a work camp to dig ditches, but later returned to the village, spending a total of seven years there.<ref>{{cite news|last1=Demick|first1=Barbara|last2=Pierson|first2=David|date=14 February 2012|title=China's political star Xi Jinping is a study in contrasts|newspaper=[[Toronto Star]]|url=https://www.thestar.com/news/world/2012/02/14/chinas_political_star_xi_jinping_is_a_study_in_contrasts.html|url-status=live|access-date=24 October 2017|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20171013225355/https://www.thestar.com/news/world/2012/02/14/chinas_political_star_xi_jinping_is_a_study_in_contrasts.html|archive-date=13 October 2017}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |author=Matt Rivers |date=19 March 2018 |title=This entire Chinese village is a shrine to Xi Jinping |work=[[CNN]] |publisher= |url=https://www.cnn.com/2018/03/19/asia/china-xi-jinping-village-intl/index.html |access-date=11 February 2021}}</ref>\n\nThe misfortunes and suffering of his family in his early years hardened Xi's view of politics. During an interview in 2000, he said, \"People who have little contact with power, who are far from it, always see these things as mysterious and novel. But what I see is not just the superficial things: the power, the flowers, the glory, the applause. I see the bullpens and how people can blow hot and cold. I understand politics on a deeper level.\" The \"bullpens\" ([[:zh:\u725b\u68da (\u6587\u9769\u7528\u8bed)|\u725b\u68da]]) was a reference to [[Red Guards]]' detention houses during the Cultural Revolution.<ref name=\":29\">{{Cite news|last=Page|first=Jeremy|date=23 December 2020|title=How the U.S. Misread China's Xi: Hoping for a Globalist, It Got an Autocrat|language=en-US|work=[[The Wall Street Journal]]|url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/xi-jinping-globalist-autocrat-misread-11608735769|access-date=25 December 2020|issn=0099-9660}}</ref>\n\nAfter being rejected seven times, Xi joined the [[Communist Youth League of China]] in 1971 by befriending a local official.<ref name=\"newyorker2\">{{cite news |last1=Osnos |first1=Evan |author-link=Evan Osnos |date=30 March 2015 |title=Born Red |magazine=[[The New Yorker]] |url=http://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2015/04/06/born-red |url-status=live |access-date=11 September 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191004153259/https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2015/04/06/born-red |archive-date=4 October 2019}}</ref> He reunited with his father in 1972, because of a family reunion ordered by [[Premier of the People's Republic of China|premier]] [[Zhou Enlai]].<ref name=\":10\" /> From 1973, he applied to join the CCP ten times and was finally accepted on his tenth attempt in 1974.<ref>{{cite news |date=28 February 2018 |title=\"The Current\" \u2013 Wednesday February 28, 2018 Full Text Transcript |work=[[CBC Radio]] |publisher= |url=http://www.cbc.ca/radio/thecurrent/the-current-for-february-28-2018-1.4555132/wednesday-february-28-2018-full-text-transcript-1.4556381#segment3 |url-status=live |access-date=28 October 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180324194159/http://www.cbc.ca/radio/thecurrent/the-current-for-february-28-2018-1.4555132/wednesday-february-28-2018-full-text-transcript-1.4556381#segment3 |archive-date=24 March 2018}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://docs.google.com/viewer?a=v&q=cache:Zy479_jx11YJ:www.aerospaceindia.org/Issue%2520Briefs/2010/25%2520October%25202010%2520-%2520CHINA%25E2%2580%2599S%2520NEXT%2520CHAIRMAN%25E2%2580%2594XI%2520JINPING.pdf+&hl=en&pid=bl&srcid=ADGEESjpQjUAtfkdEgw0cxv3UMunFfoLBcy0_po2aPN4xvPoQ-K9lFpIdgtf7PAw5ebYoTd8NwaRSswzfWmws278UPehKckL3Zb0TXlci2R0HsZGceCYRpBiD6EfBrspdjUh8CZzwcMg&sig=AHIEtbRjgFp8BCiHybWUtaP2uVF8BVuVZA |title=China's Next Chairman \u2013 Xi Jinping |last=Ranade|first=Jayadva|date=25 October 2010|publisher=Centre for Air Power Studies|access-date=27 May 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130724125447/https://docs.google.com/viewer?a=v&q=cache%3AZy479_jx11YJ%3Awww.aerospaceindia.org%2FIssue%2520Briefs%2F2010%2F25%2520October%25202010%2520-%2520CHINA%25E2%2580%2599S%2520NEXT%2520CHAIRMAN%25E2%2580%2594XI%2520JINPING.pdf+&hl=en&pid=bl&srcid=ADGEESjpQjUAtfkdEgw0cxv3UMunFfoLBcy0_po2aPN4xvPoQ-K9lFpIdgtf7PAw5ebYoTd8NwaRSswzfWmws278UPehKckL3Zb0TXlci2R0HsZGceCYRpBiD6EfBrspdjUh8CZzwcMg&sig=AHIEtbRjgFp8BCiHybWUtaP2uVF8BVuVZA |archive-date=24 July 2013|url-status=live}}</ref> From 1975 to 1979, Xi studied [[chemical engineering]] at [[Tsinghua University]] as a [[Worker-Peasant-Soldier student|worker-peasant-soldier student]] in Beijing. The engineering majors there spent about 15 percent of their time studying [[Maoism|Marxism\u2013Leninism\u2013Mao Zedong thought]] and 5 percent of their time doing farm work and \"learning from the People's Liberation Army\".{{sfn|Simon|Cong|pp=28\u201329|2009}}\n\n==Rise to power==\nFrom 1979 to 1982, Xi served as secretary for his father's former subordinate [[Geng Biao]], the then [[Vice Premier of the State Council of the People's Republic of China|vice premier]] and secretary-general of the CMC.<ref name=\"newyorker2\" /> In 1982, he was sent to [[Zhengding County]] in Hebei as deputy party secretary of Zhengding County. He was promoted in 1983 to secretary, becoming the top official of the county.{{cn|date=November 2022}} Xi subsequently served in four provinces during his regional political career: Hebei (1982\u20131985), [[Fujian]] (1985\u20132002), [[Zhejiang]] (2002\u20132007), and Shanghai (2007).<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.straitstimes.com/asia/east-asia/xi-jinping-from-graft-fighting-governor-to-chinas-most-powerful-leader-since-mao|title=Xi Jinping: From graft-fighting governor to China's most powerful leader since Mao Zedong|date=11 March 2018|newspaper=[[The Straits Times]]|language=en|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190818052957/https://www.straitstimes.com/asia/east-asia/xi-jinping-from-graft-fighting-governor-to-chinas-most-powerful-leader-since-mao|archive-date=18 August 2019|access-date=18 August 2019}}</ref> Xi held posts in the [[Fuzhou]] Municipal Party Committee and became the president of the Party School in Fuzhou in 1990. In 1997, he was named an alternate member of the [[15th Central Committee of the Chinese Communist Party|15th Central Committee of the CCP]]. However, of the 151 alternate members of the Central Committee elected at the 15th Party Congress, Xi received the lowest number of votes in favour, placing him last in the rankings of members, ostensibly due to his status as a [[Princelings|princeling]].{{NoteTag|[[Liu Yandong]], [[Wang Qishan]], and [[Deng Pufang]] (Deng Xiaoping's son) all placed among the bottom of the alternate member list. Like Xi, all three were seen as \"princelings\". [[Bo Xilai]] was not elected to the Central Committee at all; that is, Bo placed lower in the vote count than Xi.}}<ref name=\"canyu2\">{{cite news |url=http://www.canyu.org/n24349c6.aspx |script-title=zh:\u4e2d\u5171\u5341\u4e94\u5927\u4e60\u8fd1\u5e73\u4f4d\u5217\u5019\u8865\u59d4\u5458\u6700\u540e\u4e00\u540d\u4e3a\u4f55 |date=22 April 2011|website=Canyu.org|access-date=7 September 2019|language=zh-Hans|trans-title=Why Is Xi Jinping, At the 15th CCP National Congress, Being Waited On To Fill The Last Committee Member Position?|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180612141334/http://www.canyu.org/n24349c6.aspx|archive-date=12 June 2018|url-status=usurped|title=usurped title }}</ref>\n\nFrom 1998 to 2002, Xi studied [[Marxism|Marxist]] theory and ideological education in Tsinghua University,<ref>{{cite web|url=http://en.people.cn/90001/90776/6288506.html|title=Xi Jinping \u2013 Politburo Standing Committee member of CPC Central Committee |date=22 October 2007|website=[[People's Daily]]|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150630190420/http://en.people.cn/90001/90776/6288506.html|archive-date=30 June 2015|access-date=30 October 2019}}</ref> graduating with a doctorate in law and ideology in 2002.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2007/oct/26/china.uknews4|title=Most corrupt officials are from poor families but Chinese royals have a spirit that is not dominated by money|last=Watts|first=Jonathan|date=26 October 2007|newspaper=[[The Guardian]]|access-date=11 June 2008|location=London, England|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130901014357/http://www.theguardian.com/world/2007/oct/26/china.uknews4|archive-date=1 September 2013|url-status=live}}</ref> In 1999, he was promoted to the office of Vice Governor of Fujian, and became governor a year later. In Fujian, Xi made efforts to attract investment from Taiwan and to strengthen the private sector of the provincial economy.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://thediplomat.com/2014/11/from-fujian-chinas-xi-offers-economic-olive-branch-to-taiwan/|title=From Fujian, China's Xi Offers Economic Olive Branch to Taiwan|last=Tiezzi|first=Shannon|date=4 November 2014|newspaper=[[The Diplomat]]|language=en-US|url-access=limited|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160610145941/https://thediplomat.com/2014/11/from-fujian-chinas-xi-offers-economic-olive-branch-to-taiwan/|archive-date=10 June 2016|access-date=17 March 2016}}</ref> In February 2000, he and then-provincial party secretary [[Chen Mingyi]] were called before the top members of PSC&nbsp;\u2013 general secretary [[Jiang Zemin]], premier [[Zhu Rongji]], vice president [[Hu Jintao]] and [[Central Commission for Discipline Inspection|Discipline Inspection]] secretary [[Wei Jianxing]]&nbsp;\u2013 to explain aspects of the [[Yuanhua scandal]].<ref name=\"scmpxiao2\">{{cite news|url=http://www.scmp.com/article/308424/fujian-leaders-face-beijing-top-brass|title=Fujian leaders face Beijing top brass|last=Yu|first=Xiao|date=18 February 2000|newspaper=[[South China Morning Post]]|access-date=7 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20181029030831/https://www.scmp.com/article/308424/fujian-leaders-face-beijing-top-brass|archive-date=29 October 2018|url-status=live}}</ref>\n\nIn 2002, Xi left Fujian and took up leading political positions in neighbouring Zhejiang. He eventually took over as provincial Party Committee secretary after several months as acting governor, occupying a top provincial office for the first time in his career. In 2002, he was elected a full member of the [[16th Central Committee of the Chinese Communist Party|16th Central Committee]], marking his ascension to the national stage. While in Zhejiang, Xi presided over reported growth rates averaging 14% per year.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/article/1068825/chinas-leadership-transition-xi-jinpings-time-zhejiang|title=Xi Jinping's time in Zhejiang: doing the business|last=Ho|first=Louise|date=25 October 2012|newspaper=[[South China Morning Post]]|language=en|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160429160349/http://www.scmp.com/news/china/article/1068825/chinas-leadership-transition-xi-jinpings-time-zhejiang|archive-date=29 April 2016|access-date=9 December 2019}}</ref> His career in Zhejiang was marked by a tough and straightforward stance against corrupt officials. This earned him a name in the national media and drew the attention of China's top leaders.<ref name=\"wanglei2\">{{cite news|url=http://opinion.dwnews.com/news/2014-12-25/59626005.html |script-title=zh:\u4e60\u8fd1\u5e73\u4e3a\u5b98\u4e4b\u9053 \u62ce\u7740\u4e4c\u7eb1\u5e3d\u5e72\u4e8b|last1=Wang|first1=Lei|date=25 December 2014|work=[[Duowei News]]|access-date=7 September 2019|language=zh-hans|trans-title=Xi Jinping's Governmental Path \u2013 Carries Official Administrative Posts|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150203225014/http://opinion.dwnews.com/news/2014-12-25/59626005.html|archive-date=3 February 2015}}</ref>\n\nFollowing the dismissal of Shanghai Party secretary [[Chen Liangyu]] in September 2006 due to a [[Shanghai pension scandal|social security fund scandal]], Xi was transferred to Shanghai in March 2007, where he was the party secretary there for seven months.<ref name=\"shanghai2\">{{cite news|url=http://news.sohu.com/20070324/n248946143.shtml|script-title=zh:\u4e60\u8fd1\u5e73\u4efb\u4e0a\u6d77\u5e02\u59d4\u4e66\u8bb0 \u97e9\u6b63\u4e0d\u518d\u4ee3\u7406\u5e02\u59d4\u4e66\u8bb0|date=24 March 2007|work=[[Sohu]]|access-date=7 September 2019|language=zh-Hans|trans-title=Xi Jinping is Secretary of Shanghai Municipal Party Committee \u2013 Han Zheng is No Longer Acting Party Secretary|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20071016061557/http://news.sohu.com/20070324/n248946143.shtml|archive-date=16 October 2007|url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |date=15 November 2012 |title=China new leaders: Xi Jinping heads line-up for politburo |language=en-GB |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20322288 |url-status=live |access-date=25 August 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190909102154/https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20322288 |archive-date=9 September 2019}}</ref> In Shanghai, Xi avoided controversy and was known for strictly observing party discipline. For example, Shanghai administrators attempted to earn favour with him by arranging a special train to shuttle him between Shanghai and [[Hangzhou]] for him to complete handing off his work to his successor as Zhejiang party secretary [[Zhao Hongzhu]]. However, Xi reportedly refused to take the train, citing a loosely enforced party regulation that stipulated that special trains can only be reserved for \"national leaders\".<ref name=\"ming2\">{{cite news|url=http://www.boxun.com/news/gb/china/2013/07/201307110449.shtml#.VTxXVvnF_tU|script-title=zh:\u4ece\u4e0a\u6d77\u5230\u5317\u4eac \u4e60\u8fd1\u5e73\u8d34\u8eab\u79d8\u4e66\u53ea\u6709\u949f\u7ecd\u519b|date=11 July 2013|work=[[Mingjing News]]|access-date=12 September 2019|language=zh-hans|trans-title=From Shanghai to Beijing, Zhong Shaojun Has Been Xi Jinping's Only Personal Secretary|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180612141343/http://www.boxun.com/news/gb/china/2013/07/201307110449.shtml#.VTxXVvnF_tU|archive-date=12 June 2018|url-status=live}}</ref> While in Shanghai, he worked on preserving unity of the local party organisation. He pledged there would be no 'purges' during his administration, despite the fact many local officials were thought to have been implicated in the Chen Liangyu corruption scandal.{{sfn|Lam|2015|p=56}} On most issues, Xi largely echoed the line of the central leadership.<ref name=\"newin2\">{{cite news|url=http://www.caijing.com.cn/2007-10-22/100034588.html |script-title=zh:\u65b0\u664b\u653f\u6cbb\u5c40\u5e38\u59d4\uff1a\u4e60\u8fd1\u5e73|date=22 October 2007|work=[[Caijing]]|access-date=12 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20151222175301/http://www.caijing.com.cn/2007-10-22/100034588.html|archive-date=22 December 2015|language=zh-hans|trans-title=Newly Appointed Member of Politburo Standing Committee: Xi Jinping}}</ref>\n\n==Politburo Standing Committee member==\n[[File:President George W. Bush with Vice President Xi Jinping.jpg|thumb|Xi Jinping greeting U.S. president [[George W. Bush]] in August 2008|left]]\n[[File:Dmitry Medvedev in China 28 September 2010-6.jpeg|thumb|Xi Jinping with Russian president [[Dmitry Medvedev]] on 28 September 2010|left]]\n\nXi was appointed to the nine-man PSC at the [[17th National Congress of the Chinese Communist Party|17th Party Congress]] in October 2007. He was ranked above [[Li Keqiang]], an indication that he was going to succeed Hu Jintao as China's [[Succession of power in the People's Republic of China|next leader]]. In addition, Xi also held the first secretary of the CCP's [[Secretariat of the Chinese Communist Party|Central Secretariat]]. This assessment was further supported at the [[11th National People's Congress]] in March 2008, when Xi was elected as vice president of the PRC.<ref>{{Cite web |date=16 March 2008 |title=Wen Jiabao re-elected China PM |url=https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2008/3/16/wen-jiabao-re-elected-china-pm |access-date=13 August 2022 |website=[[Al Jazeera]] |language=en}}</ref> Following his elevation, Xi held a broad range of portfolios. He was put in charge of the comprehensive preparations for the [[2008 Summer Olympics]] in Beijing, as well as being the central government's leading figure in Hong Kong and Macau affairs. In addition, he also became the new president of the [[Central Party School of the Chinese Communist Party|Central Party School]] of the CCP, its cadre-training and ideological education wing. In the wake of the [[2008 Sichuan earthquake]], Xi visited disaster areas in [[Shaanxi]] and [[Gansu]]. He made his first foreign trip as vice president to North Korea, Mongolia, Saudi Arabia, Qatar and Yemen from 17 to 25 June 2008.<ref>{{cite web |date=5 June 2008 |title=Vice-President Xi Jinping to Visit DPRK, Mongolia, Saudi Arabia, Qatar and Yemen |url=http://www.mfa.gov.cn/eng/wjdt/wsrc/t462294.htm |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110723214209/http://www.mfa.gov.cn/eng/wjdt/wsrc/t462294.htm |archive-date=23 July 2011 |access-date=20 October 2010 |website=[[Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the People's Republic of China|Ministry of Foreign Affairs]] |publisher=}}</ref> After the Olympics, Xi was assigned the post of committee chair for the preparations of the [[60th Anniversary of the People's Republic of China|60th Anniversary Celebrations]] of the founding of the PRC. He was also reportedly at the helm of a top-level CCP committee dubbed the [[6521 Project]], which was charged with ensuring social stability during a series of politically sensitive anniversaries in 2009.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2009/03/10/world/asia/10china.html|title=China's Leaders See a Calendar Full of Anniversaries, and Trouble|last=Wines|first=Michael|date=9 March 2009|work=[[The New York Times]]|access-date=8 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160721045913/http://www.nytimes.com/2009/03/10/world/asia/10china.html|archive-date=21 July 2016|language=en-US|issn=0362-4331|url-access=limited}}</ref>\n\nXi's position as the apparent successor to become the paramount leader was threatened with the rapid rise of [[Bo Xilai]], the party secretary of [[Chongqing]] at the time. Bo was expected to join the PSC at the [[18th National Congress of the Chinese Communist Party|18th Party Congress]], with most expecting that he would try to eventually maneuver himself into replacing Xi.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.ft.com/content/d67b90f0-d140-11e1-8957-00144feabdc0 |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/oFuva |archive-date=11 December 2022 |url-access=subscription |url-status=live|title=Bo Xilai: power, death and politics|last=Anderlini|first=Jamil|date=20 July 2012|work=[[Financial Times]]|access-date=11 January 2020}}</ref> Bo's policies in Chongqing inspired imitations throughout China and received praise from Xi himself during Xi's visit to Chongqing in 2010. Records of praises from Xi were later erased after he became paramount leader. Bo's downfall would come with the [[Wang Lijun incident]], which opened the door for Xi to come to power without challengers.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://foreignpolicy.com/2017/10/19/the-resistible-rise-of-xi-jinping/|title=The Resistible Rise of Xi Jinping|last=Palmer|first=James|date=19 October 2017|work=[[Foreign Policy (magazine)|Foreign Policy]]|access-date=11 January 2020}}</ref>\n\nXi is considered one of the most successful members of the Princelings, a quasi-clique of politicians who are descendants of early Chinese Communist revolutionaries. Former prime minister of Singapore, [[Lee Kuan Yew]], when asked about Xi, said he felt he was \"a thoughtful man who has gone through many trials and tribulations\".<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.newsweek.com/xi-jinping-chinas-new-boss-and-l-word-95021|title=Xi Jinping: China's New Boss And The 'L' Word|last=Ansfield|first=Jonathan|date=22 December 2007|work=[[Newsweek]]|access-date=20 October 2010|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20100330064319/http://www.newsweek.com/id/81558|archive-date=30 March 2010}}</ref> Lee also commented: \"I would put him in the [[Nelson Mandela]] class of persons. A person with enormous emotional stability who does not allow his personal misfortunes or sufferings affect his judgment. In other words, he is impressive\".<ref>{{cite news|url=http://china.blogs.time.com/2007/11/19/chinas_nelson_mandela/|title=China's Nelson Mandela|last=Elegant|first=Simon|date=19 November 2007|magazine=[[Time (magazine)|Time]]|access-date=8 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090728171010/http://china.blogs.time.com/2007/11/19/chinas_nelson_mandela/|archive-date=28 July 2009|url-status=live}}</ref> Former U.S. Treasury Secretary [[Henry Paulson]] described Xi as \"the kind of guy who knows how to get things over the goal line\".<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bloomberg.com/apps/news?pid=20601087&sid=aDpLoVnlKn6E&refer=home|title=China Appoints Xi Vice President, Heir Apparent to Hu|last=Elegant|first=Simon|date=15 March 2008|newspaper=[[Bloomberg News]]|url-access=limited|access-date=20 October 2010}}</ref> Australian prime minister [[Kevin Rudd]] said that Xi \"has sufficient reformist, party and military background to be very much his own man\".<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.theaustralian.com.au/national-affairs/rudd-seeks-to-pre-empt-pms-china-white-paper-with-his-own-version/story-fn59niix-1226488505695|title=Rudd seeks to pre-empt PM's China white paper with his own version|last=Uren|first=David|date=5 October 2012|work=[[The Australian]]|access-date=8 September 2019|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20121122083821/http://www.theaustralian.com.au/national-affairs/rudd-seeks-to-pre-empt-pms-china-white-paper-with-his-own-version/story-fn59niix-1226488505695|archive-date=22 November 2012}}</ref>\n\n===Trips as Vice President===\nIn February 2009, in his capacity as vice-president, Xi Jinping embarked on a tour of Latin America, visiting Mexico,<ref>{{Cite news|url=http://en.people.cn/90001/90776/90883/6589461.html|title=Chinese vice president arrives in Mexico for official visit|date=10 February 2009|work=[[People's Daily]]|access-date=11 September 2019}}</ref> Jamaica,<ref name=\":0\">{{cite news |date=15 February 2009 |title=Chinese VP Receives Key to the City of Montego Bay |publisher=Jamaica Information Service |url=http://jis.gov.jm/chinese-vp-receives-key-to-the-city-of-montego-bay/ |url-status=live |access-date=7 September 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190714065907/https://jis.gov.jm/chinese-vp-receives-key-to-the-city-of-montego-bay/ |archive-date=14 July 2019}}</ref> Colombia,<ref>{{cite web|url=http://en.people.cn/90001/90776/90883/6594636.html|title=Chinese vice president continues visit in Colombia|date=17 February 2009|website=[[People's Daily]]|access-date=11 September 2019}}</ref> Venezuela,<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.cctv.com/english/20090218/112381.shtml|title=Chinese VP praises friendly cooperation with Venezuela, Latin America|agency=Xinhua|date=18 February 2009|work=CCTV|access-date=20 October 2022}}</ref> Brazil,<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.china.org.cn/international/2009-02/20/content_17311463.htm|title=Xi Jinping proposes efforts to boost cooperation with Brazil |date=20 February 2009|website=[[China Daily]]|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110206063031/http://china.org.cn/international/2009-02/20/content_17311463.htm|archive-date=6 February 2011|access-date=11 September 2019}}</ref> and Malta, after which he returned to China.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://timesofmalta.com/articles/view/chinese-vice-president-begins-official-visit.245968|title=Chinese vice president begins official visit|date=22 February 2009|newspaper=[[Times of Malta]]|language=en-gb|access-date=11 September 2019}}</ref> On 11 February 2009, while visiting Mexico, Xi spoke in front of a group of [[overseas Chinese]] and explained China's contributions during the international financial crisis, saying that it was \"the greatest contribution towards the whole of human race, made by China, to prevent its 1.3&nbsp;billion people from hunger\".{{NoteTag|Original {{zh|s=\u5728\u56fd\u9645\u91d1\u878d\u98ce\u66b4\u4e2d\uff0c\u4e2d\u56fd\u80fd\u57fa\u672c\u89e3\u51b313\u4ebf\u4eba\u53e3\u5403\u996d\u7684\u95ee\u9898\uff0c\u5df2\u7ecf\u662f\u5bf9\u5168\u4eba\u7c7b\u6700\u4f1f\u5927\u7684\u8d21\u732e|t=\u5728\u570b\u969b\u91d1\u878d\u98a8\u66b4\u4e2d\uff0c\u4e2d\u570b\u80fd\u57fa\u672c\u89e3\u6c7a13\u5104\u4eba\u53e3\u5403\u98ef\u7684\u554f\u984c\uff0c\u5df2\u7d93\u662f\u5c0d\u5168\u4eba\u985e\u6700\u5049\u5927\u7684\u8ca2\u737b}}|}} He went on to remark: \"There are some bored foreigners, with full stomachs, who have nothing better to do than point fingers at us. First, China doesn't export revolution; second, China doesn't export hunger and poverty; third, China doesn't come and cause you headaches.  What more is there to be said?\"{{NoteTag|Original: {{zh|s=\u6709\u4e9b\u5403\u9971\u6ca1\u4e8b\u5e72\u7684\u5916\u56fd\u4eba\uff0c\u5bf9\u6211\u4eec\u7684\u4e8b\u60c5\u6307\u624b\u753b\u811a\u3002\u4e2d\u56fd\u4e00\u4e0d\u8f93\u51fa\u9769\u547d\uff0c\u4e8c\u4e0d\u8f93\u51fa\u9965\u997f\u548c\u8d2b\u56f0\uff0c\u4e09\u4e0d\u6298\u817e\u4f60\u4eec\uff0c\u8fd8\u6709\u4ec0\u4e48\u597d\u8bf4\u7684\uff1f|t=\u6709\u4e9b\u5403\u98fd\u6c92\u4e8b\u5e72\u7684\u5916\u570b\u4eba\uff0c\u5c0d\u6211\u5011\u7684\u4e8b\u60c5\u6307\u624b\u756b\u8173\u3002\u4e2d\u570b\u4e00\u4e0d\u8f38\u51fa\u9769\u547d\uff0c\u4e8c\u4e0d\u8f38\u51fa\u98e2\u9913\u548c\u8ca7\u56f0\uff0c\u4e09\u4e0d\u6298\u9a30\u4f60\u5011\uff0c\u9084\u6709\u4ec0\u9ebd\u597d\u8aaa\u7684\uff1f}}}}<ref>{{cite news|url=http://news.asiaone.com/News/the%2BStraits%2BTimes/Story/A1Story20090214-121872.html|title=Chinese VP blasts meddlesome foreigners|last=Sim|first=Chi Yin|date=14 February 2009|newspaper=[[AsiaOne]]|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110711171035/http://news.asiaone.com/News/the%2BStraits%2BTimes/Story/A1Story20090214-121872.html|archive-date=11 July 2011|access-date=20 October 2010}}</ref> The story was reported on some local television stations. The news led to a flood of discussions on Chinese Internet forums and it was reported that the Chinese Ministry of Foreign Affairs was caught off-guard by Xi's remarks, as the actual video was shot by some accompanying Hong Kong reporters and broadcast on Hong Kong TV, which then turned up on various Internet video websites.<ref>{{Cite news|url=http://udn.com/NEWS/MAINLAND/MAI1/4742366.shtml |script-title=zh:\u7fd2\u8fd1\u5e73\u51fa\u8a2a\u7f75\u8001\u5916 \u5916\u4ea4\u90e8\u634f\u51b7\u6c57|last1=Lai|first1=Jinhong|date=18 February 2009|newspaper=[[United Daily News]]|access-date=27 February 2009|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090221023838/http://udn.com/NEWS/MAINLAND/MAI1/4742366.shtml|archive-date=21 February 2009|language=zh-hans|trans-title=Xi Jinping Goes and Scolds at Foreigners, Ministry of Foreign Affairs in Cold Sweat}}</ref>\n[[File:Xi Jinping Iowa house.jpg|thumb|right|The Muscatine, Iowa house where Xi stayed with an American family during a 1985 agricultural visit to the United States. The home was turned into a museum decades later.]]\nIn the [[European Union]], Xi visited Belgium, Germany, Bulgaria, Hungary and Romania from 7 to 21 October 2009.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.china-un.ch/eng/xwdt/t622138.htm|title=A Journey of Friendship, Cooperation and Culture \u2013 Vice Foreign Minister Zhang Zhijun Sums Up Chinese Vice President Xi Jinping's Trip to 5 European Countries|date=21 October 2009|publisher=[[Permanent Representative of China to the United Nations]]|access-date=11 September 2019}}</ref> He visited Japan, South Korea, Cambodia, and Myanmar on his Asian trip from 14 to 22 December 2009.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.southasiaanalysis.org/papers36/paper3566.html|title=China's Cousin-Cousin Relations with Myanmar|last=Raman|first=B.|date=25 December 2009|publisher=[[South Asia Analysis Group]]|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20100317074826/http://www.southasiaanalysis.org/papers36/paper3566.html|archive-date=17 March 2010|access-date=14 February 2012}}</ref> He later visited the United States, Ireland and Turkey in February 2012. This visit included meeting with then U.S. president [[Barack Obama]] at the White House<ref>{{Cite news |last1=Bull |first1=Alister |last2=Buckley |first2=Chris |date=24 January 2012 |title=China leader-in-waiting Xi to visit White House next month |work=[[Reuters]] |publisher= |url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-usa-china-xi-idUSTRE80N06E20120124 |access-date=28 October 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131029201614/https://www.reuters.com/article/2012/01/24/us-usa-china-xi-idUSTRE80N06E20120124 |archive-date=29 October 2013}}</ref> and then vice president [[Joe Biden]]; and stops in California and [[Iowa]], where he met with the family that previously hosted him during his 1985 tour as a [[Hebei]] provincial official.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2012/02/16/world/asia/xi-jinping-of-china-makes-a-return-trip-to-iowa.html|title=Xi Jinping of China Makes a Return Trip to Iowa|last=Johnson|first=Kirk|date=15 February 2012|work=[[The New York Times]]|access-date=12 September 2019|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190621114147/https://www.nytimes.com/2012/02/16/world/asia/xi-jinping-of-china-makes-a-return-trip-to-iowa.html|archive-date=21 June 2019|language=en-US|issn=0362-4331|url-access=limited}}</ref>\n\n==Leadership==\n{{Main|Xi Jinping Administration}}\n\n===Accession to top posts===\n{{See also|Generations of Chinese leadership|Succession of power in China}}\nA few months before his ascendancy to the party leadership, Xi disappeared from official media coverage and cancelled meeting with foreign officials for several weeks beginning on 1 September 2012, causing rumors.<ref name=\"newyorker2\" /> He then reappeared on 15 September.<ref>{{Cite magazine |last=Beech |first=Hannah |date=15 September 2012 |title=China's Heir Apparent Xi Jinping Reappears in Public After a Two-Week Absence |language=en-US |magazine=[[Time (magazine)|Time]] |url=https://world.time.com/2012/09/15/chinas-heir-apparent-xi-jinping-reappears-in-public-after-a-two-week-absence/ |access-date=20 August 2022 |issn=0040-781X}}</ref> On 15 November 2012, Xi was elected to the posts of general secretary of the CCP and chairman of the CMC by the [[18th Central Committee of the Chinese Communist Party|18th Central Committee of the CCP]]. This made him, informally, the [[paramount leader]] and the first to be born after the founding of the PRC. The following day Xi led the new line-up of the PSC  onto the stage in their first public appearance.<ref name=\"BBCconfirm2\">{{cite news |date=17 November 2012 |title=China Confirms Leadership Change |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-20322288 |access-date=15 November 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160729201558/http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-20322288 |archive-date=29 July 2016}}</ref> The PSC was reduced from nine to seven, with only Xi and Li Keqiang retaining their seats; the other five members were new.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.hindustantimes.com/world/xi-jinping-china-s-princeling-new-leader/story-uKjI60ipqpRSEew3Zi7jzL.html|title=Xi Jinping: China's 'princeling' new leader|date=15 November 2012|newspaper=[[Hindustan Times]]|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20181028230023/https://www.hindustantimes.com/world/xi-jinping-china-s-princeling-new-leader/story-uKjI60ipqpRSEew3Zi7jzL.html|archive-date=28 October 2018|access-date=28 October 2018}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/15/world/asia/communists-conclude-party-congress-in-china.html?ref=asia&_r=0|title=Ending Congress, China Presents New Leadership Headed by Xi Jinping|last1=Wong|first1=Edward|date=14 November 2012|work=[[The New York Times]]|access-date=16 November 2012|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20121208103006/http://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/15/world/asia/communists-conclude-party-congress-in-china.html?ref=asia&_r=0|archive-date=8 December 2012|author-link=Edward Wong|url-access=limited}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last1=FlorCruz |first1=Jaime A |last2=Mullen |first2=Jethro |date=16 November 2012 |title=After months of mystery, China unveils new top leaders |work=[[CNN]] |publisher= |url=http://edition.cnn.com/2012/11/14/world/asia/china-leadership-transition/index.html |url-status=live |access-date=16 November 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20121115195006/http://edition.cnn.com/2012/11/14/world/asia/china-leadership-transition/index.html |archive-date=15 November 2012}}</ref> In a marked departure from the common practice of Chinese leaders, Xi's first speech as general secretary was plainly worded and did not include any political slogans or mention his predecessors.<ref name=\"nytimes22\">{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/16/world/asia/new-chinese-leader-offers-few-hints-of-a-shift-in-direction.html?pagewanted=all|title=A Promise to Tackle China's Problems, but Few Hints of a Shift in Path|last1=Johnson|first1=Ian|date=15 November 2012|work=[[The New York Times]]|access-date=16 July 2014|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140726121506/http://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/16/world/asia/new-chinese-leader-offers-few-hints-of-a-shift-in-direction.html?pagewanted=all|archive-date=26 July 2014|url-access=limited}}</ref> Xi mentioned the aspirations of the average person, remarking, \"Our people ... expect better education, more stable jobs, better income, more reliable social security, medical care of a higher standard, more comfortable living conditions, and a more beautiful environment.\" Xi also vowed to tackle corruption at the highest levels, alluding that it would threaten the CCP's survival; he was reticent about far-reaching economic reforms.<ref>{{cite news |date=15 November 2012 |title=Full text: China's new party chief Xi Jinping's speech |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20338586 |url-status=live |access-date=7 September 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190914232334/https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20338586 |archive-date=14 September 2019}}</ref>\n\nIn December 2012, Xi visited [[Guangdong]] in his first trip outside Beijing since taking the Party leadership. The overarching theme of the trip was to call for further economic reform and a strengthened military. Xi visited the statue of Deng Xiaoping and his trip was described as following in the footsteps of [[Deng Xiaoping#Resignation and 1992 southern tour|Deng's own southern trip in 1992]], which provided the impetus for further economic reforms in China after conservative party leaders stalled many of Deng's reforms in the aftermath of the [[1989 Tiananmen Square protests and massacre]]. On his trip, Xi consistently alluded to his signature slogan the \"Chinese Dream\". \"This dream can be said to be the dream of a strong nation. And for the military, it is a dream of a strong military\", Xi told sailors.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424127887324128504578348774040546346|title=New Beijing Leader's 'China Dream'|last=Page|first=Jeremy|date=13 March 2013|newspaper=[[The Wall Street Journal]]|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191011195356/https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424127887324128504578348774040546346|archive-date=11 October 2019|access-date=8 September 2019}}</ref> Xi's trip was significant in that he departed from the established convention of Chinese leaders' travel routines in multiple ways. Rather than dining out, Xi and his entourage ate regular hotel buffet. He travelled in a large van with his colleagues rather than a fleet of limousines, and did not restrict traffic on the parts of the highway he travelled.<ref name=\"bbc2\">{{cite news |last1=Chen |first1=Zhuang |date=10 December 2012 |title=The symbolism of Xi Jinping's trip south |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20662947 |url-status=live |access-date=22 July 2014 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140708094339/http://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20662947 |archive-date=8 July 2014}}</ref>\n\nXi was elected president on 14 March 2013, in a confirmation vote by the [[12th National People's Congress]] in Beijing. He received 2,952 for, one vote against, and three abstentions.<ref name=\"BBCconfirm2\" /> He replaced Hu Jintao, who retired after serving two terms.<ref name=\"Demick2\">{{cite news|url=http://www.latimes.com/news/world/worldnow/la-fg-wn-china-xi-presidency-20130313,0,1240600.story|title=China's Xi Jinping formally assumes title of president|last=Demick|first=Barbara|date=3 March 2013|newspaper=[[Los Angeles Times]]|access-date=16 March 2013|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130316021753/http://www.latimes.com/news/world/worldnow/la-fg-wn-china-xi-presidency-20130313,0,1240600.story|archive-date=16 March 2013|author-link=Barbara Demick}}</ref> In his new capacity as president, on 16 March 2013 Xi expressed support for non-interference in [[China\u2013Sri Lanka relations]] amid a [[United Nations Security Council]] vote to condemn that country over government abuses during the [[Sri Lankan Civil War]].<ref>{{cite news |date=16 March 2013 |title=China's Xi Jinping hints at backing Sri Lanka against UN resolution |work=[[NDTV]] |publisher= |agency=[[Press Trust of India]] |url=http://www.ndtv.com/article/world/china-s-xi-jinping-hints-at-backing-sri-lanka-against-un-resolution-343378 |url-status=live |access-date=16 March 2013 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130316222016/http://www.ndtv.com/article/world/china-s-xi-jinping-hints-at-backing-sri-lanka-against-un-resolution-343378 |archive-date=16 March 2013}}</ref> On 17 March, Xi and his new ministers arranged a meeting with the [[Leung Chun-ying as Chief Executive of Hong Kong|chief executive of Hong Kong]], [[CY Leung]], confirming his support for Leung.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/article/1192587/cy-leung-meet-xi-jinping-beijing-and-explain-cross-border-policies|title=CY Leung to meet Xi Jinping in Beijing and explain cross-border policies|last1=Cheung|first1=Tony|date=17 March 2013|work=[[South China Morning Post]]|access-date=16 March 2013|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130318073700/http://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/article/1192587/cy-leung-meet-xi-jinping-beijing-and-explain-cross-border-policies|archive-date=18 March 2013|last2=Ho|first2=Jolie}}</ref> Within hours of his election, Xi discussed [[cyber security]] and North Korea with U.S. President Barack Obama over the phone. Obama announced the visits of treasury and state secretaries [[Jacob Lew]] and [[John F. Kerry]] to China the following week.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://newsinfo.inquirer.net/373967/china-names-xi-jinping-as-new-president|title=China names Xi Jinping as new president|date=15 March 2013 |agency=[[Agence France-Presse]] |website=Inquirer.net |access-date=16 March 2013|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130317020402/http://newsinfo.inquirer.net/373967/china-names-xi-jinping-as-new-president|archive-date=17 March 2013|url-status=live}}</ref>\n\n===Anti-corruption campaign===\n{{main|Anti-corruption campaign under Xi Jinping}}\nXi vowed to crack down on corruption almost immediately after he ascended to power at the 18th Party Congress. In his inaugural speech as general secretary, Xi mentioned that fighting corruption was one of the toughest challenges for the party.<ref name=\"inaug2\">{{cite news |date=15 November 2012 |title=Xi Jinping's inaugural Speech |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20338586 |url-status=live |access-date=7 September 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190914232334/https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-20338586 |archive-date=14 September 2019}}</ref> A few months into his term, Xi outlined the [[Eight-point Regulation]], listing rules intended to curb corruption and waste during official party business; it aimed at stricter discipline on the conduct of party officials. Xi also vowed to root out \"tigers and flies\", that is, high-ranking officials and ordinary party functionaries.<ref name=\"elite2\">{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2013/03/28/world/asia/xi-jinping-imposes-austerity-measures-on-chinas-elite.html?pagewanted=all&_r=0|title=Elite in China Face Austerity Under Xi's Rule|last=Jacobs|first=Andrew|date=27 March 2013|work=[[The New York Times]]|access-date=7 September 2019|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20181130054920/https://www.nytimes.com/2013/03/28/world/asia/xi-jinping-imposes-austerity-measures-on-chinas-elite.html?pagewanted=all&_r=0|archive-date=30 November 2018|author-link=Andrew Jacobs (journalist)}}</ref>\n\nXi initiated cases against former CMC vice-chairmen [[Xu Caihou]] and [[Guo Boxiong]], former PSC member and security chief [[Zhou Yongkang]] and former Hu Jintao chief aide [[Ling Jihua]].<ref name=\"bloom2\">{{cite news|url=https://www.bloomberg.com/news/2014-03-03/china-s-xi-broadens-graft-crackdown-to-boost-influence.html|title=President Xi's Anti-Corruption Campaign Biggest Since Mao|last=Oster|first=Shai|date=4 March 2014|publisher=[[Bloomberg News]]|access-date=7 September 2019|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141209164917/http://www.bloomberg.com/news/2014-03-03/china-s-xi-broadens-graft-crackdown-to-boost-influence.html|archive-date=9 December 2014|author-link=Shai Oster}}</ref> Along with new disciplinary chief [[Wang Qishan]], Xi's [[Xi\u2013Li Administration|administration]] spearheaded the formation of \"centrally-dispatched inspection teams\" ({{lang|zh-hans|\u4e2d\u592e\u5de1\u89c6\u7ec4}}). These were essentially cross-jurisdictional squads of officials whose main task was to gain more in-depth understanding of the operations of provincial and local party organizations, and in the process, also enforce party discipline mandated by Beijing. Many of the work teams also had the effect of identifying and initiating investigations of high-ranking officials. Over one hundred provincial-ministerial level officials were implicated during a massive nationwide anti-corruption campaign. These included former and current regional officials ([[Su Rong]], [[Bai Enpei]], [[Wan Qingliang]]), leading figures of state-owned enterprises and central government organs ([[Song Lin]], [[Liu Tienan]]), and highly ranked generals in the military ([[Gu Junshan]]). In June 2014, the [[Shanxi]] provincial political establishment was decimated, with four officials dismissed within a week from the provincial party organization's top ranks. Within the first two years of the campaign alone, over 200,000 low-ranking officials received warnings, fines, and demotions.{{sfn|Heilmann|2017|pp=62\u201375}}\n\nThe campaign has led to the downfall of prominent incumbent and retired CCP officials, including members of the PSC.<ref name=\"lam20142\">{{cite news|url=http://www.jamestown.org/programs/chinabrief/single/?tx_ttnews%5Btt_news%5D=43160&tx_ttnews%5BbackPid%5D=25&cHash=d5d24d2f82c5981963c0180d300e62cd#.VOOBN_nF9uM|title=China's Soft-Power Deficit Widens as Xi Tightens Screws Over Ideology|date=5 December 2014|access-date=7 September 2019|publisher=[[Brookings Institution]]|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160304041925/http://www.jamestown.org/programs/chinabrief/single/?tx_ttnews%5Btt_news%5D=43160&tx_ttnews%5BbackPid%5D=25&cHash=d5d24d2f82c5981963c0180d300e62cd#.VOOBN_nF9uM|archive-date=4 March 2016|url-status=live}}</ref> Xi's anti-corruption campaign is seen by critics, such as ''[[The Economist]]'', as a political tool with the aim of removing potential opponents and consolidating power.<ref>{{Cite news |date=22 October 2017 |title=Charting China's 'great purge' under Xi |language=en-GB |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-41670162 |access-date=7 January 2022}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |date=11 December 2014 |title=Tiger in the net |newspaper=[[The Economist]] |url=https://www.economist.com/china/2014/12/11/tiger-in-the-net |access-date=17 August 2022 |issn=0013-0613}}</ref> Xi's establishment of a new anti-corruption agency, the National Supervision Commission, ranked higher than the supreme court, has been described by [[Amnesty International]]'s East Asia director as a \"systemic threat to human rights\" that \"places tens of millions of people at the mercy of a secretive and virtually unaccountable system that is above the law.\"<ref>{{cite news |title=China's former military chief of staff jailed for life for corruption |url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/feb/20/chinas-former-military-chief-of-staff-jailed-for-life-for-corruption |access-date=13 September 2019 |work=[[The Guardian]]|date=20 February 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191010131232/https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/feb/20/chinas-former-military-chief-of-staff-jailed-for-life-for-corruption |archive-date=10 October 2019 |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{cite news |date=20 March 2018 |title=China's anti-corruption campaign expands with new agency |work=[[BBC News]] |publisher= |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-43453769 |url-status=live |access-date=13 September 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190924060145/https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-asia-china-43453769 |archive-date=24 September 2019}}</ref>\n\nXi has overseen significant reforms of the Central Commission for Discipline Inspection (CCDI), CCP's highest [[internal control]] institution.<ref name=\"xinj\">{{cite news |date=13 June 2014 |title=31\u4e2a\u7701\u7ea7\u7eaa\u59d4\u6539\u9769\u65b9\u6848\u83b7\u6279\u590d 12\u7701\u5df2\u5b8c\u6210\u7eaa\u59d4\"\u91cd\u5efa\" |trans-title=31 Provincial Commission for Discipline Inspection Reform Plans Approved 12 Provinces Have Completed \"Reconstruction\" |work=[[Xinhua News Agency]] |url=http://news.xinhuanet.com/politics/2014-06/13/c_126612534.htm |url-status=dead |access-date=7 January 2015 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140703204141/http://news.xinhuanet.com/politics/2014-06/13/c_126612534.htm |archive-date=3 July 2014}}</ref> He and CCDI Secretary Wang Qishan further institutionalised CCDI's independence from the day-to-day operations of the CCP, improving its ability to function as a ''bona fide'' control body.<ref name=\"xinj\" /> According to ''The Wall Street Journal'', any anti-corruption punishment to officials at or above the vice ministerial level need approval from Xi.<ref name=\":21\">{{Cite news |last=Chin |first=Josh |date=15 December 2021 |title=Xi Jinping's Leadership Style: Micromanagement That Leaves Underlings Scrambling |language=en-US |work=[[The Wall Street Journal]] |url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/xi-jinpings-leadership-style-micromanagement-that-leaves-underlings-scrambling-11639582426 |access-date=13 August 2022 |issn=0099-9660}}</ref> Another article from ''The Wall Street Journal'' said that when he wants to neutralise a political rival, he asks inspectors to prepare hundreds of pages of evidence. The article also said that he sometimes authorises investigations on close associates of a high-ranking politician to replace them with his own proteges and puts political rivals in less important positions to separate them from their political bases. Reportedly, these tactics have even been used against Wang Qishan, Xi's close friend.<ref>{{Cite news |last=Wong |first=Chun Han |date=16 October 2022 |title=Xi Jinping's Quest for Control Over China Targets Even Old Friends |language=en-US |work=[[The Wall Street Journal]] |url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/xi-jinping-china-anticorruption-11665925166 |access-date=17 October 2022 |issn=0099-9660}}</ref>\n\n===Censorship===\n{{see also|Censorship in China|Internet censorship in China}}\n\nSince Xi became the CCP general secretary, censorship, including internet censorship, has been significantly stepped up.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/chinas-communist-party-unveils-new-leadership-with-no-obvious-successor-to-xi-jinping/2017/10/25/efe67876-b8fc-11e7-9b93-b97043e57a22_story.html|title=China's Xi Jinping unveils his top party leaders, with no successor in sight|last=Denyer|first=Simon|date=25 October 2017|newspaper=[[The Washington Post]]|access-date=25 October 2017|quote=Censorship has been significantly stepped up in China since Xi took power.|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190813203127/https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/chinas-communist-party-unveils-new-leadership-with-no-obvious-successor-to-xi-jinping/2017/10/25/efe67876-b8fc-11e7-9b93-b97043e57a22_story.html|archive-date=13 August 2019|url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|ref=none|url=https://www.theguardian.com/news/2018/jun/29/the-great-firewall-of-china-xi-jinpings-internet-shutdown|title=The great firewall of China: Xi Jinping's internet shutdown|last=Economy|first=Elizabeth|author-link=Elizabeth Economy|date=29 June 2018|newspaper=[[The Guardian]]|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191010172129/https://www.theguardian.com/news/2018/jun/29/the-great-firewall-of-china-xi-jinpings-internet-shutdown|archive-date=10 October 2019|access-date=4 November 2019|quote=Before Xi Jinping, the internet was becoming a more vibrant political space for Chinese citizens. But today the country has the largest and most sophisticated online censorship operation in the world.}}</ref> Chairing the 2018 China Cyberspace Governance Conference on 20 and 21 April 2018, Xi committed to \"fiercely crack down on criminal offenses including hacking, telecom fraud, and violation of citizens' privacy.\"<ref>{{cite news |date=21 April 2018 |title=Xi outlines blueprint to develop China's strength in cyberspace |work=[[Xinhua News Agency]] |publisher= |url=http://www.xinhuanet.com/english/2018-04/21/c_137127374.htm |url-status=dead |access-date=22 April 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180421162040/http://www.xinhuanet.com/english/2018-04/21/c_137127374.htm |archive-date=21 April 2018}}</ref> During a visit to Chinese state media, Xi stated that \"party-owned media must hold the family name of the party\" ({{zh|labels=no|s=\u515a\u548c\u653f\u5e9c\u4e3b\u529e\u7684\u5a92\u4f53\u5fc5\u987b\u59d3\u515a|p=}}) and that the state media \"must embody the party\u2019s will, safeguard the party\u2019s authority\".<ref>{{Cite web |last=Zhuang |first=Pinghui |date=19 February 2016 |title=China's top party mouthpieces pledge 'absolute loyalty' as president makes rare visits to newsrooms |url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/policies-politics/article/1914136/chinas-top-party-mouthpieces-pledge-absolute-loyalty |access-date=14 August 2022 |website=[[South China Morning Post]] |language=en}}</ref>\n\nHis administration has overseen more Internet restrictions imposed in China, and is described as being \"stricter across the board\" on speech than previous administrations.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.usnews.com/news/articles/2014/06/03/tiananmen-censorship-reflects-crackdown-under-xi-jinping|title=Tiananmen Censorship Reflects Crackdown Under Xi Jinping|last=Risen|first=Tom|date=3 June 2014|work=[[U.S. News & World Report]]|access-date=8 September 2019|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190411190520/https://www.usnews.com/news/articles/2014/06/03/tiananmen-censorship-reflects-crackdown-under-xi-jinping|archive-date=11 April 2019}}</ref> Xi has taken a very strong stand to control internet usage inside China, including [[Google]] and [[Facebook]],{{sfn|Bougon|pp=157\u201365|2018}} advocating Internet censorship in the country under the concept of [[Network sovereignty|internet sovereignty]].<ref name=\":6\">{{Cite news|last=Tiezzi|first=Shannon|date=24 June 2014|title=China's 'Sovereign Internet'|language=en-US|work=[[The Diplomat]]|url=https://thediplomat.com/2014/06/chinas-sovereign-internet/|url-status=live|access-date=4 August 2017|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170804053110/https://thediplomat.com/2014/06/chinas-sovereign-internet/|archive-date=4 August 2017}}</ref><ref name=\":7\">{{Cite news|last=Ford|first=Peter|date=18 December 2015|title=On Internet freedoms, China tells the world, 'leave us alone'|work=[[The Christian Science Monitor]]|url=https://www.csmonitor.com/World/Asia-Pacific/2015/1218/On-Internet-freedoms-China-tells-the-world-leave-us-alone|url-status=live|access-date=4 August 2017|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170804055927/https://www.csmonitor.com/World/Asia-Pacific/2015/1218/On-Internet-freedoms-China-tells-the-world-leave-us-alone|archive-date=4 August 2017|issn=0882-7729}}</ref> The [[censorship of Wikipedia]] has also been stringent; as of April 2019, all versions of Wikipedia have been blocked in China.<ref>{{cite news|date=14 May 2019|title=Wikipedia blocked in China in all languages|publisher=[[BBC News]]|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/technology-48269608|url-status=live|access-date=15 May 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190515001341/https://www.bbc.com/news/technology-48269608|archive-date=15 May 2019}}</ref> Likewise, the situation for users of [[Sina Weibo|Weibo]] has been described as a change from fearing that individual posts would be deleted, or at worst one's account, to fear of arrest.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/aug/06/china-xi-jinping-crackdown-liberal-academics-minor-cultural-revolution|title='It's getting worse': China's liberal academics fear growing censorship|last=Phillips|first=Tom|date=6 August 2015|work=[[The Guardian]]|access-date=8 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190918070042/https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/aug/06/china-xi-jinping-crackdown-liberal-academics-minor-cultural-revolution|archive-date=18 September 2019|url-status=live}}</ref>\n\nA law enacted in September 2013 authorized a three-year prison term for bloggers who shared more than 500 times any content considered \"defamatory\".<ref name=\"Grigg2\">{{cite news|url=http://www.afr.com/technology/social-media/how-china-stopped-its-bloggers-20150701-gi34za|title=How China stopped its bloggers|last=Grigg|first=Angus|date=4 July 2015|newspaper=[[The Australian Financial Review]]|access-date=8 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180626001441/https://www.afr.com/technology/social-media/how-china-stopped-its-bloggers-20150701-gi34za|archive-date=26 June 2018|url-status=live}}</ref> The State Internet Information Department summoned a group of influential bloggers to a seminar instructing them to avoid writing about politics, the CCP, or making statements contradicting official narratives. Many bloggers stopped writing about controversial topics, and Weibo went into decline, with much of its readership shifting to [[WeChat]] users speaking to very limited social circles.<ref name=\"Grigg2\" /> In 2017, telecommunications carriers in China were instructed by the government to block individuals' use of [[Virtual Private Network]]s (VPNs) by February 2018.<ref>{{Cite news |date=10 July 2017 |title=China Tells Carriers to Block Access to Personal VPNs by February |language=en |work=[[Bloomberg News]] |url=https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2017-07-10/china-is-said-to-order-carriers-to-bar-personal-vpns-by-february |access-date=12 August 2022}}</ref>\n\nXi has spoken out against \"[[historical nihilism]]\", meaning historical viewpoints that challenge the official line of the CCP.<ref>{{Cite news |last=Zhai |first=Chun Han Wong and Keith |date=15 June 2021 |title=China Repackages Its History in Support of Xi's National Vision |language=en-US |work=[[The Wall Street Journal]] |url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-repackages-history-xi-propaganda-communist-party-centenary-11623767590 |access-date=13 August 2022 |issn=0099-9660}}</ref> Xi said that one of the reasons for the [[Dissolution of the Soviet Union|collapse of the Soviet Union]] has been historical nihilism.<ref>{{Cite news |title=China is struggling to keep control over its version of the past |newspaper=[[The Economist]] |url=https://www.economist.com/china/2016/10/29/china-is-struggling-to-keep-control-over-its-version-of-the-past |access-date=13 August 2022 |issn=0013-0613}}</ref> The [[Cyberspace Administration of China]] (CAC) has established a telephone hotline for people to report acts of historical nihilism, while [[Toutiao]] and [[TikTok|Douyin]] urged its user to report instances of historical nihilism.<ref>{{Cite web |last=Ma |first=Josephine |date=27 April 2022 |title=Chinese web users told to report 'historical nihilism' against party line |url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/politics/article/3175604/web-users-china-told-report-posts-guilty-historical-nihilism |access-date=13 August 2022 |website=[[South China Morning Post]] |language=en}}</ref> In May 2021, the CAC reported that it removed two million online posts for historical nihilism.<ref>{{Cite web |last=Jun |first=Mai |date=11 May 2021 |title=2 million posts deleted for 'historical nihilism' as party centenary nears |url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/politics/article/3132957/china-deletes-2-million-online-posts-historical-nihilism |access-date=13 August 2022 |website=[[South China Morning Post]] |language=en}}</ref>\n\n===Consolidation of power===\n[[File:\u6297\u6218\u80dc\u522970\u5468\u5e74\u9605\u5175\u6f14\u7ec3.jpg|thumb|Portrait of Xi in Beijing, September 2015]]\n{{See also|New Zhijiang Army}}\n\nPolitical observers have called Xi the most powerful Chinese leader since Mao Zedong, especially since the ending of presidential two-term limits in 2018.<ref name=\"PhillipsGuardian2\">{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/oct/24/xi-jinping-mao-thought-on-socialism-china-constitution|title=Xi Jinping becomes most powerful leader since Mao with China's change to constitution|last=Phillips|first=Tom|date=24 October 2017|work=[[The Guardian]]|access-date=24 October 2017|issn=0261-3077|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20171024053607/https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/oct/24/xi-jinping-mao-thought-on-socialism-china-constitution|archive-date=24 October 2017|url-status=live}}</ref><ref name=\":5\">{{cite news |date=24 October 2017 |title=China elevates Xi to most powerful leader in decades |work=[[CBC News]] |publisher= |url=http://www.cbc.ca/news/world/china-congress-xi-jinping-1.4368588 |url-status=live |access-date=22 June 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180501062515/http://www.cbc.ca/news/world/china-congress-xi-jinping-1.4368588 |archive-date=1 May 2018}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.independent.ie/world-news/china-elevates-xi-jinpings-status-making-him-the-most-powerful-leader-since-mao-36256294.html|title=China elevates Xi Jinping's status, making him the most powerful leader since Mao|date=24 October 2017|work=[[Irish Independent]]|access-date=22 June 2018|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180622220327/https://www.independent.ie/world-news/china-elevates-xi-jinpings-status-making-him-the-most-powerful-leader-since-mao-36256294.html|archive-date=22 June 2018}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last1=Collins |first1=Stephen |date=9 November 2017 |title=Xi's up, Trump is down, but it may not matter |work=[[CNN]] |publisher= |url=https://edition.cnn.com/2017/11/08/politics/president-donald-trump-xi-jinping-china/index.html |url-status=live |access-date=22 June 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180623004701/https://edition.cnn.com/2017/11/08/politics/president-donald-trump-xi-jinping-china/index.html |archive-date=23 June 2018}}</ref> Xi has notably departed from the [[collective leadership]] practices of his post-Mao predecessors. He has centralised his power and created working groups with himself at the head to subvert government bureaucracy, making himself become the unmistakable central figure of the new administration.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.csmonitor.com/World/Asia-Pacific/2018/0228/Xi-for-life-China-turns-its-back-on-collective-leadership|title=Xi for life? China turns its back on collective leadership.|last1=Holtz|first1=Michael|date=28 February 2018|work=[[The Christian Science Monitor]]|access-date=23 June 2018|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180624035940/https://www.csmonitor.com/World/Asia-Pacific/2018/0228/Xi-for-life-China-turns-its-back-on-collective-leadership|archive-date=24 June 2018|url-status=live}}</ref> Beginning in 2013, the CCP under Xi has created a series of Central Leading Groups: supra-ministerial steering committees, designed to bypass existing institutions when making decisions, and ostensibly make policy-making a more efficient process. The most notable new body is the Central Leading Group for Comprehensively Deepening Reforms. It has broad jurisdiction over economic restructuring and social reforms, and is said to have displaced some of the power previously held by the State Council and its premier.<ref name=\"keck2\">{{cite news|url=https://thediplomat.com/2014/01/is-li-keqiang-being-marginalized/|title=Is Li Keqiang Being Marginalized?|last1=Keck|first1=Zachary|date=7 January 2014|work=[[The Diplomat]]|access-date=7 September 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20181029152046/https://thediplomat.com/2014/01/is-li-keqiang-being-marginalized/|archive-date=29 October 2018|url-status=live}}</ref>\n\nXi also became the leader of the [[Central Leading Group for Internet Security and Informatization]], in charge of cyber-security and Internet policy. The Third Plenum held in 2013 also saw the creation of the [[National Security Commission of the Chinese Communist Party|National Security Commission of the CCP]], another body chaired by Xi, which commentators have said would help Xi consolidate over national security affairs.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://foreignpolicy.com/2016/06/30/chinas-much-heralded-national-security-council-has-disappeared-nsc-xi-jinping/|title=China's Much-Heralded NSC Has Disappeared|last=Wuthnow|first=Joel|date=30 June 2016|newspaper=[[Foreign Policy (magazine)|Foreign Policy]]|language=en-US|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190825180323/https://foreignpolicy.com/2016/06/30/chinas-much-heralded-national-security-council-has-disappeared-nsc-xi-jinping/|archive-date=25 August 2019|access-date=25 August 2019}}</ref><ref>{{Cite journal|last=Lampton|first=David M.|date=3 September 2015|title=Xi Jinping and the National Security Commission: policy coordination and political power|journal=Journal of Contemporary China|volume=24|issue=95|pages=759\u2013777|doi=10.1080/10670564.2015.1013366|s2cid=154685098|issn=1067-0564}}</ref> In the opinion of at least one political scientist, Xi \"has surrounded himself with cadres he met while stationed on the coast, Fujian and [[Shanghai]] and in Zhejiang.\"<ref name=\"andresy15\">{{cite book |last1=Andr\u00e9sy |first1=Agn\u00e8s |title=Xi Jinping: Red China, The Next Generation |date=2015 |publisher=UPA |page=88 |isbn=9780761866015 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=F6jTCgAAQBAJ&pg=PA88}}</ref> Control of Beijing is seen as crucial to Chinese leaders; Xi has selected [[Cai Qi]], one of the cadres mentioned above, to manage the capital.<ref name=\"economistcai\">{{cite news |date=13 July 2017 |title=Xi Jinping has chosen an unusual man to lead the capital city |newspaper=[[The Economist]] |url=https://www.economist.com/china/2017/07/13/xi-jinping-has-chosen-an-unusual-man-to-lead-the-capital-city |access-date=13 August 2022}}</ref> Xi has also been believed to have diluted the authority of premier [[Li Keqiang]], taking authority over the economy which has generally been considered to be the domain of the premier.<ref>{{Cite news |date=28 September 2020 |title=Li Keqiang: China's marginalised premier |language=en-GB |work=[[BBC News]] |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-china-19870221 |access-date=17 October 2022}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Wei |first=Lingling |date=11 May 2022 |title=China's Forgotten Premier Steps Out of Xi's Shadow as Economic Fixer |language=en-US |work=[[The Wall Street Journal]] |url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-premier-li-keqiang-xi-jinping-11652277107 |access-date=17 October 2022 |issn=0099-9660}}</ref> In 2022, Xi appointed his close ally [[Wang Xiaohong]] as the Minister of Public Security, giving him further control over the security establishment.<ref>{{Cite web |last=Lau |first=Jack |date=24 June 2022 |title=Close Xi Jinping ally appointed as China's new public security chief |url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/politics/article/3183027/close-xi-jinping-ally-appointed-chinas-new-public-security |access-date=13 August 2022 |website=[[South China Morning Post]] |language=en}}</ref>\n\nIn its sixth plenary session in November 2021, CCP adopted a [[Third historical resolution|historical resolution]], a kind of document that evaluated the party's history. This was the third of its kind after ones adopted by Mao Zedong and Deng Xiaoping,<ref>{{Cite web |last=Wang |first=Cat |date=7 November 2021 |title=The significance of Xi Jinping's upcoming 'historical resolution' |url=https://www.scmp.com/news/china/politics/article/3154996/xi-jinpings-historical-resolution-what-it-and-why-it-important |access-date=11 August 2022 |website=[[South China Morning Post]] |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{Cite web |last=Ni |first=Vincent |date=11 November 2021 |title=Chinese Communist party elevates Xi's status in 'historical resolution' |url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2021/nov/11/chinese-communist-party-elevates-xi-jinping-status-historical-resolution |access-date=11 August 2022 |website=[[The Guardian]] |language=en}}</ref> and the document for the first time credited Xi as being the \"main innovator\" of Xi Jinping Thought<ref>{{Cite news |last= |date=12 November 2021 |title=China's Communist Party passes resolution amplifying President Xi's authority |language=en |work=[[Reuters]] |url=https://www.reuters.com/world/china/chinas-communist-party-passes-resolution-party-history-achievements-2021-11-11/ |access-date=11 August 2022}}</ref> while also declaring Xi's leadership as being \u201cthe key to the great rejuvenation of the Chinese nation\".<ref>{{Cite news |date=11 November 2021 |title=Chinese Communist party clears way for Xi to tighten grip on power |work=[[Financial Times]] |url=https://www.ft.com/content/77f8dd89-fd16-42f9-b2a5-0f5e9ee93ace |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/ZYz1R |archive-date=11 December 2022 |url-access=subscription |url-status=live |access-date=11 August 2022}}</ref> In comparison with the other historical resolutions, Xi's one did not herald a major change in how the CCP evaluated its history.<ref>{{Cite news |last=Zhai |first=Chun Han Wong and Keith |date=17 November 2021 |title=How Xi Jinping Is Rewriting China's History to Put Himself at the Center |language=en-US |work=[[The Wall Street Journal]] |url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-xi-jinping-is-rewriting-chinas-history-to-put-himself-at-the-center-11637157022 |access-date=11 August 2022 |issn=0099-9660}}</ref> To accompany the historical
